         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         0°
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
          Albedo
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         0.071±0.012
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jplssd_2-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-jplssd-2">
           [2]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
          Temperature
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         ≈ 233
         <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
          K
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">
           Apparent magnitude
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         11.8
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
          <a href="#cite_note-3">
           [3]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Phobos
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'f' in 'find'">
            f
           </span>
           <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
            oʊ
           </span>
           <span title="'b' in 'buy'">
            b
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          FOH
         </span>
         -bəs
        </i>
       </a>
       or
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'f' in 'find'">
            f
           </span>
           <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
            oʊ
           </span>
           <span title="'b' in 'buy'">
            b
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          FOH
         </span>
         -bos
        </i>
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       from the Greek
       <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Greek" title="Help:IPA for Greek">
         Φόβος
        </a>
       </span>
       .) (
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_naming_conventions" title="Astronomical naming conventions">
        systematic designation
       </a>
       :
       <b>
        <span class="nowrap">
         Mars I
        </span>
       </b>
       ) is the innermost and larger of the two
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">
        natural satellites
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the other being
       <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
        Deimos
       </a>
       . Both moons were discovered in 1877 by American astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Asaph_Hall" title="Asaph Hall">
        Asaph Hall
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos is a small, irregularly shaped object with a mean radius of 11 km (7 mi),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_1-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is seven times as massive as the outer moon, Deimos. Phobos is named after the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek god
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(mythology)" title="Phobos (mythology)">
        Phobos
       </a>
       , a son of
       <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">
        Ares
       </a>
       (Mars) and
       <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">
        Aphrodite
       </a>
       (Venus), and the personification of
       <a href="/wiki/Horror_and_terror" title="Horror and terror">
        horror
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Cf." title="Cf.">
        cf.
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Phobia" title="Phobia">
        phobia
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos orbits 6,000 km (3,700 mi) from the Martian surface, closer to its
       <a href="/wiki/Primary_(astronomy)" title="Primary (astronomy)">
        primary body
       </a>
       than any other known planetary moon. It is indeed so close that it orbits Mars much faster than Mars rotates, and completes an orbit in just 7 hours and 39 minutes. As a result, from the surface of Mars it appears to rise in the west, move across the sky in 4 hours and 15 minutes or less, and set in the east, twice each
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_day" title="Martian day">
        Martian day
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos is one of the least reflective bodies in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       , with an
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       of just 0.071. Surface temperatures range from about −4 °C (25 °F) on the sunlit side to −112 °C (−170 °F) on the shadowed side.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phobos_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-phobos-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The defining surface feature is the large
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact crater
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stickney_(crater)" title="Stickney (crater)">
        Stickney
       </a>
       , which takes up a substantial proportion of the moon's surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       Images and models indicate that Phobos may be a
       <a href="/wiki/Rubble_pile" title="Rubble pile">
        rubble pile
       </a>
       held together by a thin
       <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">
        crust
       </a>
       , and that it is being torn apart by
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">
        tidal
       </a>
       interactions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falling_apart_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Falling_apart-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Phobos gets closer to Mars by about 2 meters every one hundred years, and it is predicted that within 30 to 50 million years it will either collide with the planet, or break up into a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">
        planetary ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phobos_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-phobos-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars#History" title="Moons of Mars">
        History of the moons of Mars
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Phobos was discovered by astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Asaph_Hall" title="Asaph Hall">
        Asaph Hall
       </a>
       on 18 August 1877, at the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">
        United States Naval Observatory
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">
        Washington, D.C.
       </a>
       , at about 09:14
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">
        Greenwich Mean Time
       </a>
       (contemporary sources, using the pre-1925 astronomical convention that began the day at noon, give the time of discovery as 17 August at 16:06
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_mean_time" title="Washington mean time">
        Washington mean time
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morley1989_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Morley1989-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hall had discovered
       <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
        Deimos
       </a>
       , Mars's other moon, a few days earlier on 12 August 1877 at about 07:48 UTC. The names, originally spelled
       <i>
        Phobus
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Deimus
       </i>
       respectively, were suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_George_Madan" title="Henry George Madan">
        Henry Madan
       </a>
       (1838–1901), Science Master of
       <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">
        Eton
       </a>
       , based on Greek mythology, in which Phobos is a companion to the god
       <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">
        Ares
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2661" data-file-width="1488" height="393" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg/220px-PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg/330px-PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg/440px-PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA21858-MarsMoon-Phobos-ThermalIR-20170929.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Phobos - surface temperatures: 130 to 270 K (-226 to 26°F) (
         <a href="/wiki/Thermal_Emission_Imaging_System" title="Thermal Emission Imaging System">
          THEMIS
         </a>
         ; 29 September 2017)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Phobos has dimensions of
       <span class="nowrap">
        27 km × 22 km × 18 km
       </span>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_1-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and retains too little mass to be rounded under its own gravity. Phobos does not have an
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       due to its low mass and low gravity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is one of the least reflective bodies in the Solar System, with an albedo of about 0.071.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jplssd_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-jplssd-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Infrared spectra show that it is lacking in carbon-rich material found in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrites" title="Carbonaceous chondrites">
        carbonaceous chondrites
       </a>
       . Instead, its composition shows similarities to that of Mars’ surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Phobos's density is too low to be solid rock, and it is known to have significant
       <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">
        porosity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_Inspection_for_Phobos_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Close_Inspection_for_Phobos-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These results led to the suggestion that Phobos might contain a substantial reservoir of ice. Spectral observations indicate that the surface
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       layer lacks hydration,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but ice below the regolith is not ruled out.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos is heavily cratered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most prominent of these is the crater,
       <a href="/wiki/Stickney_(crater)" title="Stickney (crater)">
        Stickney
       </a>
       , (named after Asaph Hall's wife,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angeline_Stickney_Hall" title="Angeline Stickney Hall">
        Angeline Stickney Hall
       </a>
       , Stickney being her maiden name) a large impact crater some 9 km (5.6 mi) in diameter, taking up a substantial proportion of the moon's surface area. As with
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       's crater
       <a href="/wiki/Herschel_(Mimantean_crater)" title="Herschel (Mimantean crater)">
        Herschel
       </a>
       , the impact that created Stickney must have nearly shattered Phobos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many grooves and streaks also cover the oddly shaped surface. The grooves are typically less than 30 meters (98 ft) deep, 100 to 200 meters (330 to 660 ft) wide, and up to 20 kilometers (12 mi) in length, and were originally assumed to have been the result of the same impact that created Stickney. Analysis of results from the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_Express" title="Mars Express">
         Mars Express
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft, however, revealed that the grooves are not in fact radial to Stickney, but are centered on the leading apex of Phobos in its orbit (which is not far from Stickney). Researchers suspect that they have been excavated by material ejected into space by impacts on the surface of Mars. The grooves thus formed as
       <a href="/wiki/Crater_chain" title="Crater chain">
        crater chains
       </a>
       , and all of them fade away as the trailing apex of Phobos is approached. They have been grouped into 12 or more families of varying age, presumably representing at least 12 Martian impact events.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grooves_origin_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-grooves_origin-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Faint dust rings produced by Phobos and Deimos have long been predicted but attempts to observe these rings have, to date, failed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recent images from
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">
         Mars Global Surveyor
        </a>
       </i>
       indicate that Phobos is covered with a layer of fine-grained
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       at least 100 meters thick; it is hypothesized to have been created by impacts from other bodies, but it is not known how the material stuck to an object with almost no gravity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The unique
       <a href="/wiki/Kaidun_meteorite" title="Kaidun meteorite">
        Kaidun meteorite
       </a>
       that fell on a
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet
       </a>
       military base in
       <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">
        Yemen
       </a>
       in 1980 has been hypothesized to be a piece of Phobos, but this has been difficult to verify because little is known about the exact composition of Phobos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 68 kg (150 lb) person standing on the surface of Phobos would weigh the equivalent to about 60 g (2 oz) on Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Named_geological_features">
        Named geological features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Named geological features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Geological features on Phobos are named after
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">
        astronomers
       </a>
       who studied Phobos and people and places from
       <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">
        Jonathan Swift
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">
         Gulliver's Travels
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is one named
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_geology#Regio_(astronomy)" title="Planetary geology">
        regio
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Laputa Regio
       </i>
       , and one named
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planitia" title="Planitia">
        planitia
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Lagado Planitia
       </i>
       ; both are named after places in
       <i>
        Gulliver's Travels
       </i>
       (the fictional
       <a href="/wiki/Laputa" title="Laputa">
        Laputa
       </a>
       , a flying island, and
       <a href="/wiki/Lagado" title="Lagado">
        Lagado
       </a>
       , imaginary capital of the fictional nation
       <a href="/wiki/Balnibarbi" title="Balnibarbi">
        Balnibarbi
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The only named ridge on Phobos is
       <i>
        Kepler Dorsum
       </i>
       , named after the astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
        Johannes Kepler
       </a>
       . Several craters have been named.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:right;">
      </div>

           <img alt="" data-file-height="2999" data-file-width="5499" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png/500px-USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png/750px-USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png/1000px-USGS-Phobos-MarsMoon-Map.png 2x" width="500"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
         <i>
          Left:
         </i>
         The impact crater
         <a href="/wiki/Stickney_(crater)" title="Stickney (crater)">
          Stickney
         </a>
         imaged by the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">
           Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter
          </a>
         </i>
         in March 2008. The second impact crater inside Stickney is
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limtoc" title="Limtoc">
          Limtoc
         </a>
         .
         <i>
          Right:
         </i>
         Labeled Map of Phobos – Moon of Mars (U.S. Geological Survey).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhobosMap_33-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-PhobosMap-33">
           [33]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_characteristics">
        Orbital characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Orbital characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbits_of_Phobos_and_Deimos.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="160" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Orbits_of_Phobos_and_Deimos.gif" width="160"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orbits_of_Phobos_and_Deimos.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Orbits of Phobos and
         <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
          Deimos
         </a>
         . Phobos makes about four orbits for every one made by
         <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
          Deimos
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_motion" title="Orbital motion">
        orbital motion
       </a>
       of Phobos has been intensively studied, making it "the best studied natural satellite in the Solar System" in terms of orbits completed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bills_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bills-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its close orbit around Mars produces some unusual effects. With an altitude of 5,989 km (3,721 mi), Phobos orbits Mars below the
       <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">
        synchronous orbit
       </a>
       radius, meaning that it moves around Mars faster than Mars itself rotates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Close_Inspection_for_Phobos_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Close_Inspection_for_Phobos-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore, from the point of view of an observer on the surface of Mars, it rises in the west, moves comparatively rapidly across the sky (in 4 h 15 min or less) and sets in the east, approximately twice each Martian day (every 11 h 6 min). Because it is close to the surface and in an
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        equatorial
       </a>
       orbit, it cannot be seen above the horizon from
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        latitudes
       </a>
       greater than 70.4°. Its orbit is so low that its
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">
        angular diameter
       </a>
       , as seen by an observer on Mars, varies visibly with its position in the sky. Seen at the horizon, Phobos is about 0.14° wide; at
       <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">
        zenith
       </a>
       it is 0.20°, one-third as wide as the full
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       as seen from
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       . By comparison, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       has an apparent size of about 0.35° in the Martian sky. Phobos's phases, inasmuch as they can be observed from Mars, take 0.3191 days (Phobos's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synodic" title="Synodic">
        synodic
       </a>
       period) to run their course, a mere 13 seconds longer than Phobos's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_period" title="Sidereal period">
        sidereal period
       </a>
       . As seen from Phobos, Mars would appear 6,400 times larger and 2,500 times brighter than the full
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       appears from Earth, taking up a quarter of the width of a celestial hemisphere. The Mars–Phobos
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagrange_1_point" title="Lagrange 1 point">
        Lagrangian L1
       </a>
       is 2.5 kilometers (1.6 mi) above Stickney, which is unusually close to the surface.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_transits">
        Solar transits
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Solar transits">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Phobos_from_Mars" title="Transit of Phobos from Mars">
        Transit of Phobos from Mars
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="120" data-file-width="360" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg/220px-PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg/330px-PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17356-MarsCuriosityRover-EclipseOfSunByPhobos.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annular_eclipse" title="Annular eclipse">
          Annular eclipse
         </a>
         of the Sun by Phobos as viewed by the Mars
         <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">
          <i>
           Curiosity
          </i>
          rover
         </a>
         (20 August 2013).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       An observer situated on the Martian surface, in a position to observe Phobos, would see regular
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">
        transits
       </a>
       of Phobos across the Sun. Several of these transits have been photographed by the Mars Rover
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MER-B" title="MER-B">
         Opportunity
        </a>
       </i>
       . During the transits, Phobos's shadow is cast on the surface of Mars; an event which has been photographed by several spacecraft. Phobos is not large enough to cover the Sun's disk, and so cannot cause a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_eclipse" title="Total eclipse">
        total eclipse
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Predicted_destruction">
        Predicted destruction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predicted destruction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_deceleration" title="Tidal deceleration">
        Tidal deceleration
       </a>
       is gradually decreasing the orbital radius of Phobos by 2 meters every one hundred years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falling_apart_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Falling_apart-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Scientists estimate that Phobos will be destroyed in approximately 30–50 million years,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falling_apart_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Falling_apart-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bills_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bills-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with one study's estimate being about 43 million years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Efroimsky_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Efroimsky-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos' grooves were long thought to be fractures caused by the impact that formed the
       <a href="/wiki/Stickney_(crater)" title="Stickney (crater)">
        Stickney crater
       </a>
       . Other modelling suggested since the 1970s support the idea that the grooves are more like "stretch marks" that occur when Phobos gets deformed by tidal forces, but in 2015 when the tidal forces were calculated and used in a new model, the stresses were too weak to fracture a solid moon of that size, unless Phobos is a rubble pile surrounded by a layer of powdery regolith about 100 m (330 ft) thick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falling_apart_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Falling_apart-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Given Phobos's irregular shape and assuming that it is a pile of rubble (specifically a
       <a href="/wiki/Mohr%E2%80%93Coulomb_theory" title="Mohr–Coulomb theory">
        Mohr–Coulomb body
       </a>
       ), it will eventually break up when it reaches approximately 2.1 Mars radii.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-death-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Researchers suggest that the grooves are "stretch marks" caused by tidal forces. This idea is based on the model that Phobos is a rubble pile surrounded by a 100 m (330 ft) layer of powdery regolith. Stress fractures calculated for this model line up with the grooves on Phobos. The model is supported with the discovery that some of the grooves are younger than others, implying that the process that produces the grooves is ongoing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When Phobos is eventually torn apart by tidal forces, it is likely that a fraction of the debris will form a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring_system_(astronomy)" title="Ring system (astronomy)">
        planetary ring
       </a>
       around Mars. This ring may last for between one million and one hundred million years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
        Origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv/854px--MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="89.187845804989" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8c/MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv/MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv.720p.webm" type="video/webm;          <img alt="File:MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv/220px--MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MarsCuriosityRover-PhobosEclipsesSun-20130820.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Video (01:30/
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Real_time_(media)" title="Real time (media)">
           real-time
          </a>
         </i>
         ):
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Mars" title="Solar eclipses on Mars">
          <i>
           Eclipse
          </i>
          of the Sun
         </a>
         by
         <i>
          Phobos
         </i>
         , largest of the two
         <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">
          Moons of Mars
         </a>
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curiosity_rover" title="Curiosity rover">
          Curiosity rover
         </a>
         , 20 August 2013).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The origin of the Martian moons is still controversial.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burns_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-burns-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Phobos and Deimos both have much in common with carbonaceous
       <a href="/wiki/C-type_asteroid" title="C-type asteroid">
        C-type asteroids
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">
        spectra
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       very similar to those of C- or D-type asteroids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c-type_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-c-type-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on their similarity, one hypothesis is that both moons may be captured
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-belt_asteroids" title="Main-belt asteroids">
        main-belt asteroids
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landis_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-landis-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both moons have very circular orbits which lie almost exactly in Mars's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_plane" title="Equatorial plane">
        equatorial plane
       </a>
       , and hence a capture origin requires a mechanism for circularizing the initially highly eccentric orbit, and adjusting its inclination into the equatorial plane, most probably by a combination of atmospheric drag and
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">
        tidal forces
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cazenave_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cazenave-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although it is not clear that sufficient time is available for this to occur for Deimos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burns_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-burns-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Capture also requires dissipation of energy. The current Martian atmosphere is too thin to capture a Phobos-sized object by atmospheric braking.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burns_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-burns-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Landis" title="Geoffrey Landis">
        Geoffrey Landis
       </a>
       has pointed out that the capture could have occurred if the original body was a
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_asteroid" title="Binary asteroid">
        binary asteroid
       </a>
       that separated under tidal forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-landis_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-landis-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:245px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17352-MarsMoons-PhobosPassesDeimos-RealTime.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="156" data-file-width="243" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/PIA17352-MarsMoons-PhobosPassesDeimos-RealTime.gif" width="243"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17352-MarsMoons-PhobosPassesDeimos-RealTime.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">
           Curiosity's
          </a>
         </i>
         view of the
         <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">
          Mars moons
         </a>
         : Phobos passing in front of
         <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
          Deimos
         </a>
         – in
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Real_time_(media)" title="Real time (media)">
           real-time
          </a>
         </i>
         (video-gif, 1 August 2013).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Phobos could be a second-generation Solar System object that
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
        coalesced
       </a>
       in orbit after Mars formed, rather than forming concurrently out of the same birth cloud as Mars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA2010_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ESA2010-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another hypothesis is that Mars was once surrounded by many Phobos- and Deimos-sized bodies, perhaps ejected into orbit around it by a collision with a large
       <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">
        planetesimal
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craddock_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Craddock-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The high porosity of the interior of Phobos (based on the density of 1.88 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       , voids are estimated to comprise 25 to 35 percent of Phobos's volume) is inconsistent with an asteroidal origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andert_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Andert-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Observations of Phobos in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">
        thermal infrared
       </a>
       suggest a composition containing mainly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phyllosilicates" title="Phyllosilicates">
        phyllosilicates
       </a>
       , which are well known from the surface of Mars. The spectra are distinct from those of all classes of
       <a href="/wiki/Chondrite" title="Chondrite">
        chondrite
       </a>
       meteorites, again pointing away from an asteroidal origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giuranna_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Giuranna-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both sets of findings support an origin of Phobos from material ejected by an impact on Mars that reaccreted in Martian orbit,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blast_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Blast-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       similar to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" title="Giant impact hypothesis">
        prevailing theory
       </a>
       for the origin of Earth's moon.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span id="Shklovsky.27s_.22Hollow_Phobos.22_hypothesis">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Shklovsky's_&quot;Hollow_Phobos&quot;_hypothesis">
        Shklovsky's "Hollow Phobos" hypothesis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Shklovsky's &quot;Hollow Phobos&quot; hypothesis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In the late 1950s and 1960s, the unusual orbital characteristics of Phobos led to speculations that it might be hollow.
      </p>
      <p>
       Around 1958, Russian astrophysicist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iosif_Samuilovich_Shklovsky" title="Iosif Samuilovich Shklovsky">
        Iosif Samuilovich Shklovsky
       </a>
       , studying the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular_phenomena" title="Secular phenomena">
        secular
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">
        acceleration
       </a>
       of Phobos's orbital motion, suggested a "thin sheet metal" structure for Phobos, a suggestion which led to speculations that Phobos was of artificial origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shklovsky_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shklovsky-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Shklovsky based his analysis on estimates of the upper Martian atmosphere's density, and deduced that for the weak braking effect to be able to account for the secular acceleration, Phobos had to be very light—one calculation yielded a hollow iron sphere 16 kilometers (9.9 mi) across but less than 6 cm thick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shklovsky_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shklovsky-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In a February 1960 letter to the journal
       <i>
        Astronautics
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singer_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Singer-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Singer" title="Fred Singer">
        Fred Singer
       </a>
       , then science advisor to U.S. President
       <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">
        Dwight D. Eisenhower
       </a>
       , said of Shklovsky's theory:
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Globe_of_Phobos.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1318" data-file-width="1757" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Globe_of_Phobos.JPG/220px-Globe_of_Phobos.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Globe_of_Phobos.JPG/330px-Globe_of_Phobos.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Globe_of_Phobos.JPG/440px-Globe_of_Phobos.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Globe_of_Phobos.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Globe of Phobos at the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorial_Museum_of_Astronautics" title="Memorial Museum of Astronautics">
          Memorial Museum of Astronautics
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">
          Moscow
         </a>
         (19 May 2012).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        If the satellite is indeed spiraling inward as deduced from astronomical observation, then there is little alternative to the hypothesis that it is hollow and therefore Martian made. The big 'if' lies in the astronomical observations; they may well be in error. Since they are based on several independent sets of measurements taken decades apart by different observers with different instruments, systematic errors may have influenced them.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singer_54-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Singer-54">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Subsequently, the systemic data errors that Singer predicted were found to exist, and the claim was called into doubt,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Öpik1963_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Öpik1963-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and accurate measurements of the orbit available by 1969 showed that the discrepancy did not exist.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Singer's critique was justified when earlier studies were discovered to have used an overestimated value of 5 cm/yr for the rate of altitude loss, which was later revised to 1.8 cm/yr.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The secular acceleration is now attributed to tidal effects,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Öpik1963_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Öpik1963-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which had not been considered in the earlier studies.
      </p>
      <p>
       The density of Phobos has now been directly measured by spacecraft to be 1.887 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dlr2008_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dlr2008-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Current observations are consistent with Phobos being a rubble pile.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dlr2008_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-dlr2008-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, images obtained by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking_probes" title="Viking probes">
        <i>
         Viking
        </i>
        probes
       </a>
       in the 1970s clearly showed a natural object, not an artificial one. Nevertheless, mapping by the
       <i>
        Mars Express
       </i>
       probe and subsequent volume calculations do suggest the presence of voids and indicate that it is not a solid chunk of rock but a porous body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">
        porosity
       </a>
       of Phobos was calculated to be 30% ± 5%, or a quarter to a third being empty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andert_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Andert-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Launched_missions">
        Launched missions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Launched missions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:512px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phobos_Viewed_from_Mars.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="228" data-file-width="700" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Phobos_Viewed_from_Mars.jpg/510px-Phobos_Viewed_from_Mars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Phobos_Viewed_from_Mars.jpg 1.5x" width="510"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phobos_Viewed_from_Mars.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Phobos slowly came into view over the decades as it was imaged at progressively higher resolutions. This image compares images from the Mars
         <a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)">
          <i>
           Spirit
          </i>
          rover
         </a>
         and an orbiter (2005).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phobos_Marte.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="420" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Phobos_Marte.jpg/220px-Phobos_Marte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Phobos_Marte.jpg/330px-Phobos_Marte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Phobos_Marte.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phobos_Marte.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Illustration of the
         <a href="/wiki/Phobos_program" title="Phobos program">
          Phobos probe
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Phobos has been photographed in close-up by several spacecraft whose primary mission has been to photograph Mars. The first was
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mariner_7" title="Mariner 7">
         Mariner 7
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1969, followed by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Viking_1" title="Viking 1">
         Viking 1
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1977,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">
         Mars Global Surveyor
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1998 and 2003,
       <i>
        Mars Express
       </i>
       in 2004, 2008, and 2010,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mars_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter">
         Mars Reconnaissance Orbiter
        </a>
       </i>
       in 2007 and 2008. On August 25, 2005, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_Rover" title="Spirit Rover">
        Spirit Rover
       </a>
       , with an excess of energy due to wind blowing dust off of its solar panels, took several short-exposure photographs of the night sky from the surface of Mars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Phobos and Deimos are both clearly visible in the photograph.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Soviet Union undertook the
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_program" title="Phobos program">
        Phobos program
       </a>
       with two probes, both launched successfully in July 1988.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Phobos_1" title="Phobos 1">
         Phobos 1
        </a>
       </i>
       was accidentally shut down by an erroneous command from ground control issued in September 1988 and lost while the craft was still en route.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Phobos_2" title="Phobos 2">
         Phobos 2
        </a>
       </i>
       arrived at the Mars system in January 1989 and, after transmitting a small amount of data and imagery but shortly before beginning its detailed examination of Phobos's surface, the probe abruptly ceased transmission due either to failure of the on-board computer or of the radio transmitter, already operating on the backup power. Other Mars missions collected more data, but the next dedicated mission attempt would be a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">
        sample return mission
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Space_Agency" title="Russian Space Agency">
        Russian Space Agency
       </a>
       launched a sample return mission to Phobos in November 2011, called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fobos-Grunt" title="Fobos-Grunt">
         Fobos-Grunt
        </a>
       </i>
       . The return capsule also included a life science experiment of
       <a href="/wiki/The_Planetary_Society" title="The Planetary Society">
        The Planetary Society
       </a>
       , called
       <a href="/wiki/Living_Interplanetary_Flight_Experiment" title="Living Interplanetary Flight Experiment">
        Living Interplanetary Flight Experiment
       </a>
       , or LIFE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A second contributor to this mission was the
       <a href="/wiki/China_National_Space_Administration" title="China National Space Administration">
        China National Space Administration
       </a>
       , which supplied a surveying satellite called "
       <a href="/wiki/Yinghuo-1" title="Yinghuo-1">
        Yinghuo-1
       </a>
       ", which would have been released in the orbit of Mars, and a soil-grinding and sieving system for the scientific payload of the Phobos lander.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, after achieving
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">
        Earth orbit
       </a>
       , the
       <i>
        Fobos-Grunt
       </i>
       probe failed to initiate subsequent burns that would have sent it off to Mars. Attempts to recover the probe were unsuccessful and it crashed back to Earth in January 2012.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Missions_considered">
        Missions considered
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Missions considered">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S86_25375patraw.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4475" data-file-width="3014" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/S86_25375patraw.jpg/220px-S86_25375patraw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/S86_25375patraw.jpg/330px-S86_25375patraw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/S86_25375patraw.jpg/440px-S86_25375patraw.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S86_25375patraw.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Fuel is mined from Phobos with the help of a nuclear reactor. (P. Rawlings, 1986)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
          <a href="#cite_note-67">
           [67]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1997 and 1998, the
       <i>
        Aladdin
       </i>
       mission was selected as a finalist in the NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Program" title="Discovery Program">
        Discovery Program
       </a>
       . The plan was to visit both Phobos and Deimos, and launch projectiles at the satellites. The probe would collect the ejecta as it performed a slow flyby (~1 km/s).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These samples would be returned to Earth for study three years later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Principal Investigator was Dr.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carle_Pieters" title="Carle Pieters">
        Carle Pieters
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">
        Brown University
       </a>
       . The total mission cost, including launch vehicle and operations was $247.7 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ultimately, the mission chosen to fly was
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">
         MESSENGER
        </a>
       </i>
       , a probe to Mercury.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2007, the European aerospace subsidiary
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EADS_Astrium" title="EADS Astrium">
        EADS Astrium
       </a>
       was reported to have been developing a mission to Phobos as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_demonstrator" title="Technology demonstrator">
        technology demonstrator
       </a>
       . Astrium was involved in developing a
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       plan for a sample return mission to Mars, as part of the ESA's
       <a href="/wiki/Aurora_programme" title="Aurora programme">
        Aurora programme
       </a>
       , and sending a mission to Phobos with its low gravity was seen as a good opportunity for testing and proving the technologies required for an eventual sample return mission to Mars. The mission was envisioned to start in 2016, was to last for three years. The company planned to use a "mothership", which would be propelled by an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">
        ion engine
       </a>
       , releasing a lander to the surface of Phobos. The lander would perform some tests and experiments, gather samples in a capsule, then return to the mothership and head back to Earth where the samples would be jettisoned for recovery on the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_missions">
        Proposed missions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Proposed missions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monolith55103h-crop.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="563" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Monolith55103h-crop.jpg/220px-Monolith55103h-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Monolith55103h-crop.jpg/330px-Monolith55103h-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Monolith55103h-crop.jpg/440px-Monolith55103h-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monolith55103h-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Phobos_monolith" title="Phobos monolith">
          Phobos monolith
         </a>
         (right of center) as taken by the
         <a href="/wiki/Mars_Global_Surveyor" title="Mars Global Surveyor">
          Mars Global Surveyor
         </a>
         (MOC Image 55103, 1998).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2007, the
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">
        Canadian Space Agency
       </a>
       funded a study by
       <a href="/wiki/Optech" title="Optech">
        Optech
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Mars_Institute" title="Mars Institute">
        Mars Institute
       </a>
       for an unmanned mission to Phobos known as
       <i>
        Phobos Reconnaissance and International Mars Exploration
       </i>
       (PRIME). A proposed landing site for the PRIME spacecraft is at the "
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_monolith" title="Phobos monolith">
        Phobos monolith
       </a>
       ", a prominent object near Stickney crater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The PRIME mission would be composed of an orbiter and lander, and each would carry 4 instruments designed to study various aspects of Phobos's geology.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2008,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Glenn_Research_Center" title="NASA Glenn Research Center">
        NASA Glenn Research Center
       </a>
       began studying a Phobos and Deimos sample return mission that would use solar electric propulsion. The study gave rise to the "Hall" mission concept, a
       <a href="/wiki/New_Frontiers_program" title="New Frontiers program">
        New Frontiers
       </a>
       -class mission under further study as of 2010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another concept of a sample return mission from Phobos and Deimos is
       <a href="/wiki/OSIRIS-REx#OSIRIS-REx_II" title="OSIRIS-REx">
        OSIRIS-REx II
       </a>
       , which would use heritage technology from the first
       <a href="/wiki/OSIRIS-REx" title="OSIRIS-REx">
        OSIRIS-REx
       </a>
       mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of January 2013, a new
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Phobos_Surveyor" title="Phobos Surveyor">
         Phobos Surveyor
        </a>
       </i>
       mission is currently under development by a collaboration of
       <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">
        Stanford University
       </a>
       , NASA's
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">
        Jet Propulsion Laboratory
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
        Massachusetts Institute of Technology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pandika-2012_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pandika-2012-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mission is currently in the testing phases, and the team at Stanford plans to launch the mission between 2023 and 2033.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pandika-2012_80-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pandika-2012-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In March 2014, a Discovery class mission was proposed to place an orbiter in Mars orbit by 2021 to study Phobos and Deimos through a series of close flybys. The mission is called
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_And_Deimos_%26_Mars_Environment" title="Phobos And Deimos &amp; Mars Environment">
        Phobos And Deimos &amp; Mars Environment
       </a>
       (PADME).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpsc_2014_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lpsc_2014-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reyes_2014_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Reyes_2014-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lpsc_2015_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lpsc_2015-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two other Phobos missions that were proposed for the Discovery 13 selection included a mission called
       <i>
        Merlin
       </i>
       , which would flyby Deimos but actually orbit and land on Phobos, and another one is
       <i>
        Pandora
       </i>
       which would orbit both Deimos and Phobos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tps_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tps-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japanese Aerospace Exploration Agency">
        Japanese Aerospace Exploration Agency
       </a>
       (JAXA) unveiled in 9 June 2015 the Martian Moons eXploration (MMX), a sample return mission targeting Phobos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       MMX will land and collect samples from Phobos multiple times, along with conducting Deimos flyby observations and monitoring Mars's climate. By using a
       <a href="/wiki/Core_drill" title="Core drill">
        corer
       </a>
       sampling mechanism, the spacecraft aims to retrieve a minimum 10 kg amount of samples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujimoto20170111_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fujimoto20170111-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       NASA, ESA, and
       <a href="/wiki/CNES" title="CNES">
        CNES
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are also participating in the project, and will provide scientific instruments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The U.S. will contribute the Neutron and Gamma-Ray Spectrometer (NGRS), and France the Near IR Spectrometer (NIRS4/MacrOmega).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujimoto20170111_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-fujimoto20170111-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although the mission has been selected for implementation
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and is now beyond proposal stage, formal project approval by JAXA has been postponed following the
       <a href="/wiki/Hitomi_(satellite)" title="Hitomi (satellite)">
        Hitomi
       </a>
       mishap.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Development and testing of key components, including the sampler, is currently ongoing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2017, MMX is scheduled to be launched in 2024, and will return to Earth five years later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fujimoto20170111_86-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-fujimoto20170111-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Russia plans to repeat
       <a href="/wiki/Fobos-Grunt" title="Fobos-Grunt">
        Fobos-Grunt
       </a>
       mission in the late 2020s, and the European Space Agency is assessing a sample-return mission for 2024 called
       <a href="/wiki/Phootprint" title="Phootprint">
        Phootprint
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Airbus_2014_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Airbus_2014-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COSPAR_2014_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-COSPAR_2014-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="As_part_of_a_manned_mission_to_Mars">
        As part of a manned mission to Mars
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Phobos_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: As part of a manned mission to Mars">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phobosmgs.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="683" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Phobosmgs.gif/170px-Phobosmgs.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Phobosmgs.gif/255px-Phobosmgs.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Phobosmgs.gif/340px-Phobosmgs.gif 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phobosmgs.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Phobos in 1998
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
          <a href="#cite_note-97">
           [97]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Phobos has been proposed as an early target for a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" title="Manned mission to Mars">
        manned mission to Mars
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Teleoperation" title="Teleoperation">
        teleoperation
       </a>
       of robotic scouts on Mars by humans on Phobos could be conducted without significant time delay, and
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_protection" title="Planetary protection">
        planetary protection
       </a>
       concerns in early Mars exploration might be addressed by such an approach.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Phobos has also been proposed as an early target for a manned mission to Mars because a landing on Phobos would be considerably less difficult and expensive than a landing on the surface of Mars itself. A lander bound for Mars would need to be capable of
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">
        atmospheric entry
       </a>
       and subsequent return to orbit, without any support facilities (a capacity that has never been attempted in a manned spacecraft), or would require the creation of support facilities in-situ (a "colony or bust" mission); a lander intended for Phobos could be based on equipment designed for lunar and
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       landings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, the
       <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">
        delta-v
       </a>
       to land on Phobos and return is only 80% of that for a trip to and from the surface of the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       , partly due to Phobos's very weak gravity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
          <span title="A more detailed citation is required. (July 2015)">
           full citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The human exploration of Phobos could serve as a catalyst for the human exploration of Mars and be exciting and scientifically valuable in its own right.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most recently, it was proposed that the sands of Phobos could serve as a valuable material for
       <a href="/wiki/Aerobraking" title="Aerobraking">
        aerobraking
       </a>
       in the colonization of Mars
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arias16_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-arias16-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . Because the small delta-v budget of Phobos, a small amount of chemical fuel brought from Earth could be transformed in a very large amount of sand lifted from the surface of Phobos -from a permanent outpost, to a transfer orbit. This sand could be released in front of the spacecraft during the descent maneuver and then resulting in a densification of the atmosphere just in front of the spacecraft.
      </p>
      <p>
       <br/>
      </p>
      <p>
       <br/>
      </p>

            </a>

            <span class="reference-accessdate">

             <span class="nowrap">
